Pyrene was introduced to construct new organic gain media. Lai et al. reported pyrenyl-capped benzofiurene derivatives as gain media. Pyrenyl-capped benzofiurene was selected as molecular skeleton, and functionalized with different flexible alkyl chains on diaryl groups at each C9 position of fluorene units.228,229 The selected pyrenyl-capped benzofiurene derivatives (PFP-n, n = 1–3) (83–85) are shown in Scheme 6. PFP-3 (85), with phosphonate polar pendant groups at C9 position of the fluorene units, exhibited blue-shifted emission and much higher ASE thresholds, compared with that recorded from PFP-1 (83) and PFP-2 (84). PFP-1 (83) was selected to further investigate the lasing performance due to its higher PLQY, lower ASE threshold, and better photoelectrical property. By varying the film thicknesses, the lasing wavelength could be tuned within a range of 16 nm (from 456 nm to 472 nm) in the blue spectral region. The lowest lasing threshold of 1.4 μJ cm−2 was obtained with lasing peak at 456 nm.
The spiro concept, based on the connection of two molecular π-systems through a sp3-hybridized carbon atom with equal or different functions, has been used in the design of organic gain media. The use of such a linkage has been demonstrated to effectively depress the excimer formation and the ketonic defects, increase the thermal and spectral stabilities, and thus strongly enhance PLQYs and ASE/lasing properties. Due to the facile functionalization at C-2, C-7, C-10, and C-15 positions of spirobifluorene unit, various spiro-functional materials, including small molecules (as discussed in this section and shown in Scheme 7), dendrimers (see Section 3.2.3.3), and polymers (see Section 3.2.4.1), have been explored for lasing applications.
 Scheme 7  Spirobifluorene molecules as gain media. 
For example, Spehr et al. reported the lasing performance based on an active gain medium, spiro-terphenyl (Sp-TP, 86), in a second-order DFB structure.230 Sp-TP (86) showed ASE in the UV spectral region with emission peak at 381 nm. With DFB grating periods from 200 nm to 230 nm, the lasing wavelengths of thin-film devices were operated in a range of more than 30 nm between 362 nm and 394 nm. The minimum threshold of 8.9 μJ cm−2 with peak at 383 nm was achieved under optical pumping. By increasing the π-conjugated system (e.g., adding the phenyl numbers for Sp-HBP (87)) or linking acceptor units (e.g., linking oxadiazole for SP-SPO (88)), the ASE/lasing spectra of spirobifluorene molecules showed bathochromic shifts to the blue spectral region.231,232
Spirobifluorene molecules linked with donor or acceptor groups were studied as small molecular gain media. Donor units, such as diphenylamine or carbazole units, were usually used to construct donor–π–donor structures. The film of carbazole-functionalized spirobifluorene, named as Sp-Cz (89), showed an ASE threshold of 25 μJ cm−2 with emission peak at 396 nm.233 Molecular extension with phenylene–vinylene groups (Sp-SBCz, 90) increased the π-conjugated system, resulting in bathochromic-shifted amplification with a much lower threshold of 0.43 μJ cm−2. When doped into CBP host, the Sp-SBCz (90) blend film exhibited an ASE threshold down to 0.11 μJ cm−2, which represented one of the lowest ASE thresholds for organic emitters by far. More rotational freedom could be provided for the individual phenyl rings when the end-capped carbazole units are replaced by diphenylamine groups. The respective diphenylamine-functionalized spirobifluorene is Sp-DPA (93). The stimulated emission wavelength (at 452 nm) of Sp-DPA (93) showed a red shift compared with the ASE peak of Sp-BCz (92).234 The end-capped with diphenylamine phenylene-vinylene extended spiro-bifluorene (Sp-SDPA, 91) showed an ASE threshold of 2 μJ cm−2.234
3.2.2.2 Ladder-type oligo(p-phenylene)s and carbon-bridged oligo (p-phenylenevinylene)s (COPVs). 
Ladder-type poly(p-phenylene)s (LPPP) is a vital series of polymeric material system for OSLs, which will be discussed separately in polymer gain media part (see Section 3.2.4.2). Ladder-type oligo(p-phenylene)s also have been explored as gain media most recently. Lai et al. reported a novel set of D–π–A ladder-type oligo(p-phenylene)s with functional end-cappers of diphenyl-amino (donor) and fluorophenyl/fluorine (acceptor) units, named as nL-F (94, 96, 98) and nLPh-F (95, 97, 99) (n = 2–4), as gain media for lasing applications.235 The ASE thresholds decreased with the extension of the π-conjugated system for end-capper functionalized oligo(p-phenylene)s. In 4LPh-F (99) film with the largest π-conjugated system, relatively low ASE threshold of 1.97 μJ cm−2 was recorded. Excellent optical waveguiding properties with gain coefficient higher than 90 cm−1 and loss coefficient lower than 2.0 cm−1 were also demonstrated in 4LPh-F (99) film. 4LPh-F (99) showed good photothermal stability, and ASE could still be detectable even up to 280 °C. Lasing thresholds of 2.2 μJ cm−2 and 0.5 μJ cm−2 with corresponding peaks at 460 nm and 471 nm were demonstrated in 1D DFB lasers using 4L-F (98) and 4LPh-F (99) as gain media, respectively. Carbazole was also explored as end-capper in linear ladder-type oligo(p-phenylene)s, named as 4L-Cz (100), as a deep-blue gain medium.236 Blue ASE with a low ASE threshold of 0.53 μJ cm−2 and emission peak at 446 nm was demonstrated in 4L-Cz (100) film. A high gain value of 82 cm−1 and a low loss value of 3.9 cm−1 were recorded. The best 1D DFB laser device showed a threshold of 0.17 μJ cm−2 with lasing slope efficiency up to 5.9%. A lasing wavelength tunable window of 19 nm was achieved in the blue spectral region.
COPVs is another interesting type of all carbon skeleton materials. The synthetic method was firstly reported by Zhu et al. in 2009.237 The chemical structures of COPV-n (101–106) (n = 1–6) are shown in Scheme 8. As illustrated in Fig. 10a and b for COPV4 (104), the planar π-conjugated molecular skeleton was sterically protected from aggregation and degradation by incorporating bulky p-octylphenyl units, which also enabled good miscibilities with organic solid matrixes and good solubilities in organic solvents. The absorption spectra of COPV-n (101–106) showed obvious bathochromic shifts with the increase of the π-conjugated system, indicating the effective conjugations of COPV-n (101–106) have significantly increased in this process (Fig. 10c).238 The slope efficiency between energy bandgap and the reciprocal number of repeating units (1/n) was 1.76, much higher than that recorded in ladder-type oligo(p-phenylene)s239 and oligofluorenes.240 Very broad fluorescence emission range was observed in COPV-n (101–106) with different numbers of repeating units (n) (Fig. 10d). By fabricating DFB lasers, single mode lasing of COPV-n (101–106) doped in PS film exhibited remarkably low thresholds (7 μJ cm−2), tunable wavelength from deep-blue- to red-spectral region (408–591 nm) and long operational lifetimes (>3 × 105 pump pulses for oligomers with three to six repeating units) (Fig. 10e and f).241
 Scheme 8  Ladder-type oligo(p-phenylene)s and COPVs as gain media. 
 Fig. 10  (a) Chemical structure of COPV-4 with Ar denoting 4-octylphenyl. (b) Top- and side-view of COPV-4 single-crystal X-ray analysis. (c and d) Absorption spectra (c) and fluorescence spectra (d) of COPV-1–6 in dichloromethane. (e) ASE intensity vs. the number of pump pulses and time (t) (top and bottom axes, respectively) excited under soft laser energy (SP, 2-fold ASE threshold) and extremely high laser energy (EP, 103-fold ASE threshold) conditions for a 2 wt% COPV6-doped film. (f) DFB lasing spectra based on COPV-n-doped films (n = 1–6) spread on resonators with different grating periods. (a, e and f) Reproduced with permission from ref. 241. Copyright 2015 Nature Publishing Group. (b–d) Reproduced with permission from ref. 238. Copyright 2012 American Chemical Society. 
3.2.2.3 Phenylamine and benzocarbazole derivatives. 
Triphenyldiamines, which are well-known as hole-transporting materials, have been widely used as one of the functional layers in OLEDs and OSCs. Also as an individual, triphenyldiamine is a fluorescent material with deep-blue emission. N,N′-Bis(3-methylphenyl)-N,N′-diphenylbenzidine (TPD, 107) is a well-studied triphenyldiamine derivative. When mixed TPD (107) into PS matrix with concentrations from 2.1 wt% to 70 wt%, the doped films showed tunable ASE spectra between 413 and 421 nm.242 A net gain value of 16 cm−1 was recorded for 20 wt% TPD-doped film. Spin-coated onto a DFB grating resonator with grating period of 270 nm, the 30 wt% TPD-doped film emitted lasing at 418 nm when the pumping energy density was above 537 μJ cm−2 (6 μJ per pulse).243 Other TPDs, including 1,4-bis(diphenylamino)biphenyl (DPABP, 108), N,N′-bis(3-methyl-phenyl)-N,N′-diphenyl-[1,1′:4′,1′′-terphenyl]-4,4′′-diamine (BMT-TPD, 109), N,N′-diphenyl-N,N′-bis(1-naphthyl)-1,1′-biphenyl-4,4′′-diamine (NPB, 110), and N,N′-bis(3-methylphenyl)-N,N′-diphenyl-[1,1′:4′,1′′:4′′,1′′′-quaterphenyl]-4,4′′′-diamine (BMQ-TPD, 111) (Scheme 9), were explored as doped gain media by Ma et al.244,245 Among them, BMQ-TPD (111) showed the lowest ASE threshold (42 μJ cm−2), while there was no evidence of ASE in NPB (110) doped film. Molecular geometries were calculated to simulate the four-level system in these molecules. A series of energy sublevels were estimated in NPB (110) from various elongated low-frequency modes between 11 cm−1 and 689 cm−1 deriving from naphthyl unit, which are adverse to the construction of four-level system. For comparison, the strong elongated high-frequency modes from 1199 cm−1 to 1664 cm−1 deriving from benzene unit in TPD (107), DPABP (108), BMT-TPD (109), and BMQ-TPD (111) contributed to the evident vibronic band in the fluorescent emission, indicating an efficient four-level system for stimulated emission.
 Scheme 9  Phenylamine and benzocarbarzole derivatives. 
Triarylamine is mainly used as an electron-donating unit in the applications of OLEDs and OSCs. By linking various electron-withdrawing groups onto the triphenylamino core, multi-color emission from blue to red were observed through the combination of inter-branch electronic energy transfer and intramolecular charge transfer (ICT).246 4-di(4′-tert-butylbiphenyl-4-yl)amino-4′-dicyano-vinyl-benzene (FVIN, 112), coupled to the triphenylamino core with dicyanovinyl units, was used as red-emitting organic gain medium.247 The non-doped FVIN (112) film exhibited an ASE threshold as low as 22 μJ cm−2 and an optical gain of 50 cm−1, indicating tremendously reduced quenching effects in comparison with classical dyes such as rhodamine 6G and DCM. Lasing in the red region at 647 nm with a threshold of 60 μJ cm−2 was obtained with a patterning DBR resonator. Triarylamine linked with other units, such as ethene-diyldibenzene, vinyl-carbazole, vinylquinoline, and vinylnaphthalene, named as SBD-n (113–117) (n = 1–5), were reported with ASE phenomena using CBP as the host in the guest–host system. The chemical structures of SBD-n (113–117) (n = 1–5) are depicted in Scheme 9, and the ASE properties are summarized in Table 3.248
CBP (118) is one of the most studied bipolar hosts in OLEDs and guest–host gain systems. The UV ASE were investigated by using CBP and its derivatives, 4,4′-bis(3-methylcarbazol-9-yl)-2,2′-biphenyl (mCBP, 119) and 4,4′-bis(3,6-dimethyl-carbazol-9-yl)-2,2′-biphenyl (dCBP, 120), as the emitters.249 For undoped CBP (118) films, ASE at 394 nm was observed with a threshold of 1.3 μJ cm−2. The red shifted ASE spectra centered at 401 nm and 406 nm were demonstrated in mCBP (119) and dCBP (120) films, with the corresponding thresholds of 1.7 μJ cm−2 and 1.5 μJ cm−2. The neat gains were strongly dependent on their chemical structures. CBP (118), mCBP (119), and dCBP (120) exhibited gain values of 13, 4.0, and 7.8 cm−1, respectively.
Bis-styrylbenzene derivatives are interesting laser materials as crystal gain media. The ASE characteristics of a series of bis-styrylbenzene derivatives end-capped with triarylamine and benzocarbazole (BSB-n, n = 1–3, and BSBCz, 121–124) doped into CBP host were reported by Adachi's group.248 Among them, the 6 wt% doped BSBCz (124) film demonstrated the lowest ASE threshold of 0.32 μJ cm−2. Since then, BSBCz (124) was used in quasi-CW operation of OSLs, due to its very high PLQY (nearly 100%), extremely weak population of triplet excitons via intersystem crossing, and inappreciable excited-state absorption in the lasing region.250,251 Recently, Adachi et al. reported the low-threshold quasi-CW DFB lasers (based on doped BSBCz (124) film) operating under long-pulse width of 30 ms as well as pulse repetition rate up to 80 MHz.21 CBP was used as host in the guest–host system. As shown in Fig. 11a, in order to greatly improve the DFB laser stabilities and efficiencies operating in the quasi-CW regimes, CYTOP was spin-coated onto the organic layers to encapsulate the devices. Mixed-order DFB grating was fabricated to provide a sufficient out-coupling of the laser radiation (Fig. 11b). Fig. 11c and d show the images of lasing oscillations in the encapsulated blended mixed-order DFB laser device under different pulse repetition rates captured by streak camera. The encapsulated blended mixed-order DFB laser device held the lowest thresholds (from 0.06 μJ cm−2 to 0.25 μJ cm−2) under the pulse repetition rate varying from 10 kHz to 80 MHz, and was the only live device after operating at 80 MHz (Fig. 11e). With the same tendency, a reduced lasing threshold was also observed in the encapsulated blended mixed-order DFB laser device in the quasi-CW regime at long-pulse photoexcitations (Fig. 11f and g). As shown in Fig. 11h, the ultra-high energy-induced thermal degradation of active layer led to the irreversible decreased laser intensity during operation in all devices. Noticeably, the device lifetime was greatly enhanced by encapsulation. Only 3% emission intensity decreased after 500 pulses (pulse width: 100 μs) in the encapsulated blended device. This work represented an important breakthrough toward the realization of organic solid-state CW laser technology.
 Fig. 11  (a) Chemical structures of CBP and BSBCz as host and guest materials for organic lasing. The chemical structure of CYTOP polymer for encapsulation. (b) Schematic illustration of the mixed-order DFB resonator. (c and d) The images of lasing oscillations captured by streak camera from a encapsulated doped (BSBCz concentration of 6 wt%) mixed-order DFB device with repetition rate varying from 0.01 to 80 MHz over a period of (c) 500 μs or (d) 200 ns (80 MHz only). (e) Dependence of the lasing threshold on the pulse repetition rate for several types of DFB devices. (f) The images of lasing emission captured by streak camera from an encapsulated doped (BSBCz concentration of 20 wt%) mixed-order DFB device with pulse widths of 30 ms (top) and 800 μs (bottom). And the corresponding pump energy were 2.0 kW cm−2 and 200 W cm−2, respectively. (g) Lasing threshold as a function of excitation pulse width in various DFB devices. (h) Laser intensity vs. the number of incident pulse from several types of DFB devices in the quasi-CW regime at long-pulse photoexcitation (100 μs and 200 W cm−2). Reproduced with permission from ref. 21. Copyright 2017 AAAS. 
Based on the same gain medium, an electrically pumped organic laser was reported by Adachi's group in 2019, which will be discussed further in Section 4.23
3.2.2.4 Thiophene-based small molecules. 
In Section 3.2.1, we have introduced the oligothiophene and TPCO crystals for lasing. Some thiophene-based amorphous small molecules were also reported as gain media (Scheme 10). For example, Huang et al. reported a novel material (DTBT, 125) based on the core of dithienylbenzothiadiazole and the end-cappers of 3,5-bis(1-naphthyl)-phenyl.252 DTBT (125) film exhibited ASE characteristics with a threshold of 380 μJ cm−2 and emission peak at 644 nm. DFB laser devices were fabricated by spin-coating DTBT (125) onto the grating with period of 410 nm. By varying the spin-coated film thicknesses from 410 nm to 560 nm, a lasing wavelength tunable window of 15 nm was present with the lowest lasing threshold of 118 μJ cm−2 (at 650 nm).
 Scheme 10  Thiophene-based small molecules. 
Fused thiophenes, for example, pentathienoacenes (PTA), are interesting for various applications due to the combination of the rigid planarity from acenes and the chemical stability from oligothiophenes in their chemical structures. Incorporating three PTA derivatives (PTA-1/2/3) (126–128) into host matrix PS with concentration of 10%, the doped films exhibited ASE thresholds of 100, 240 and 750 μJ cm−2 for PTA-1 (126), PTA-2 (127), and PTA-3 (128), respectively. Alternatively, guest–host gain systems were built up with incorporating PTA-2 (127) and PTA-3 (128) as guest and PTA-1 (126) as host, which yielded lower ASE thresholds than that in PS host by facilitating energy transfer from host to guest. In this way, ASE threshold of 370 μJ cm−2 with emission peak at 573 nm was observed for PTA-3 (128).253
3.2.2.5 Perylenediimide (PDI) derivatives. 
Perylenediimide (PDI) derivatives are mainly studied for OTFTs and OSCs, due to their facile chemical structure tunabilities, excellent thermal stabilities and optical stabilities, and strong electron acceptabilities.254–256 High fluorescent efficiencies (nearly 100%) of PDI monomers in dilute solution enable their applications in single molecule spectroscopy, optical sensing, and lasing. Nonetheless, in solid state, the strong π–π intermolecular interactions give rise to the formation of cofacially stacked H-aggregates in PDI molecules, quenching fluorescence emission unfavorably and strongly.257 The crystallization of PDI molecules is depressed by attaching alkyl or other bulky substituted groups, accompanying with the increased fluorescent efficiencies. PDI monomers are functionalized with various alkyl and aryl flexible solubilizing chains, e.g., PDI-C6, PDI-o, and b-PDI-C8 (129–131, Scheme 11).258–260 Incorporating N,N′-di-(1-hexylheptyl)perylene-3,4:9,10-tetracarboxylic diimide (PDI-C6, 129) into PS matrix with concentrations from 0.5 to 5 wt%, stimulated emission was determined with the optimized threshold as low as 120 μJ cm−2 at concentration of 0.5 wt% of PDI-C6 (129) in the matrix.260 The doped film onto DFB resonator manifested a lasing threshold of 160 μJ cm−2.259 The laser showed an operation lifetime of 3.1 × 105 pump pulses (more than 8 h) with pumped energy of 2.2-fold lasing threshold and pulse repetition rate of 10 Hz. The lasing characteristics of PDI-o (130) and b-PDI-C8 (131) are listed in Table 3.
 Scheme 11  Perylenediimide (PDI) derivatives. 
In another study, WGM hexagonal microdisk microcavity and nanowire of PDI molecules, named as N,N′-bis(1-ethylpropyl)-2,5,8,11-tetrakis(p-methyl-phenyl)-perylenediimide (mp-PDI-1, 132) and N,N′-bis(1-ethylpropyl)-2,5,8,11-tetrakis(o-methylphenyl)-perylene-3,4:9,10-tetraarboxylic acid diimide (mp-PDI-2, 133), were obtained via the reprecipitation method and solution self-assembly method.69,91 The hexagonal WGM microdisk of mp-PDI-1 (132) exhibited a PLQY of ca. 15%, a fast fluorescence decay time (ca. 0.05 ns), and a lasing threshold around 10.8 μJ cm−2.
3.2.2.6 Other small-molecular gain media. 
Like organic crystals, anthracene was studied as gain media for lasing applications.14,15 Since the first report on the high fluorescent efficiency and outstanding stability of 9,10-di-(2-naphthyl)anthracene,261 there are intense interests in exploring novel anthracene derivatives as promising candidates with deep-blue emission for OLEDs.262–264 Niu et al. reported two asymmetric 9,10-disubstituted anthracene molecules, named as An-Cz-1 and An-Cz-2 (134 and 135), as thin-film gain media.265 The molecular difference between An-Cz-1 (134) and An-Cz-2 (135) was the different units substituted on the anthracene 9-position, which are 3,5-bis(4-t-butylphenyl) phenyl and 3,5-bis(2,4-difluorophenyl) phenyl, respectively. An-Cz-1 (134) exhibited a lower ASE threshold (46.9 μJ cm−2vs. 68.8 μJ cm−2) and a higher gain value (23 cm−1vs. 19 cm−1) relative to An-Cz-2 (135). 1D DFB lasers of An-Cz-1 (134) with a minimum lasing threshold of 17.7 μJ cm−2 and a corresponding slope efficiency of 3.6% were achieved.
The ASE and lasing phenomena could be observed from oligophenylenes (p-nP) as crystals (see Section 3.2.1.1). But there was no gain-narrowing and light amplification even pumped with a high enough energy in p-nP thin films.266 Introducing peripheral naphthyl units onto the oligophenyls (npN, N = 1–3, 136–138) caused obvious sterical dimensions, weakened intermolecular interactions and suppressed π–π stacking. The amorphous 3pN–5pN (136–138) films showed PLQY values between 22% and 35%, manifesting common ASE performance.266
Generally, due to the notorious generation of excimers and exciplexes, most chromophores endure aggregation-caused quenching (ACQ) in solid state, even with high fluorescence efficiency in dilute solution. An inverse effect known as aggregation-induced emission (AIE) was reported by Tang and co-workers in 2001.267 Since then, the research of organic fluorophors with AIE characteristics becomes a hot topic.268 AIE molecules as the emitters generally exhibit bright emission. Cheng et al. reported a UV lasing from tetraphenylethylene (TPE, 139), which was a famous and easily functionalized AIE fluorogen, doped in PMMA polymer nanowires.269 The polymer nanowires were fabricated by template wetting in the anodic aluminum oxide channels. Output lasing at 372 nm from individual nanowire with threshold of 55 mJ cm−2 was observed at 10 K. Another AIE molecule, named as 2,5,2′,5′-tetrakis(2,2-diphenylvinyl)biphenyl (TDPVBi, 140), also shows distinct ASE behavior.270 TDPVBi (140) film fabricated by vacuum-depositing process showed high PLQY (80%) and an ASE threshold of 60 μJ cm−2. These works suggest the possibility of AIE molecules for lasing applications.
Some other molecules, such as diarylboron diketonates (DBDK-1 and DBDK-2, 141 and 142)271 and dibenzo[hi,st]ovalene (DBO, 143),272 were reported with ASE phenomena. The chemical structures of DBDK-1 (141), DBDK-2 (142), and DBO (143) are depicted in Scheme 12. The optical characteristics are summarized in Table 3.
 Scheme 12  Other small molecules as gain media. 


3.2.3 Dendritic starburst molecules. 
Dendritic starbursts are an important category of organic gain media, which are usually constituted by a central core and multiple functionalized π-conjugated arms. Combining the merits of small molecules and polymers, dendritic starburst molecules contribute to organic optoelectronics with well-defined molecular structures, good film-forming characteristics, excellent thermal- and optical-stabilities, and thus outstanding optoelectronic properties. The suppressed aggregates and self-quenching induced by the highly branched arms enable dendritic starburst molecules as promising candidates for OSLs. In the past decade, several reviews have comprehensively summarized dendritic starburst molecules and their applications for organic electronics and photonics.273–276 The recent advances in organic synthetic technologies make it possible to design and synthesize novel materials with bulky structures and complex branches, achieving excellent functional characteristics like well-defined small molecules and high-molecular-weight polymers. In this part, we confine the discussion on the recent reported starburst molecules as organic gain media and their lasing characteristics.
3.2.3.1 Oligofluorenes. 
PFO is an important commercial polymer, which has been widely used in laser physics, and regarded as the benchmark for organic gain media (see Section 3.2.4.1). Correspondingly, the fluorene oligomers, especially starburst oligofluorenes, also form an important class of organic gain media.
In 2007, Burn's group reported a series of bisfluorene-cored dendrimers for lasing applications.277,278 Different dendrimers are formed by attaching biphenyl (BP), biphenylcarbazole (BPCz), and E-stilbenyl (ESB) onto the bisfluorene core, named as BP-1 (144), BPCz (146), and ESB (147), respectively. The ASE peak occurred at 431 nm in BPCz (146), exhibiting a redshift emission compared with BP-1 (144) and ESB (147). The BP-1 (144) presented the lowest threshold with a value of 16 μJ cm−2 and the highest gain of 51 cm−1.278 With the development from the first generation (BP-1, 144) to the second generation (BP-2, 145), a rise in PLQY from 42% to 75% was observed. By using etched silica substrates DFB grating, BP-2 (145) exhibited an improvement in lasing performance compared with BP-1 (144). BP-1 (144) showed a lasing threshold of 90 nJ per pulse and a slope efficiency of 0.3%; while BP-2 (145) laser had a half lower threshold and more than 5-times higher slope efficiency. By using a soft lithographic technique called as solvent assisted micromoulding (SAMIM), a micromoulded BP-2 (145) laser with a very high slope efficiency of 14% could be achieved.277 In addition, a series of H-shaped oligofluorenes, bearing one rigid arm of diarylfluorenes and another arm of trifluorene with alkyl chains, have been developed in Huang's group.279 The H-shaped hybrid oligomers linking trifluorene, carbazole, and diarylfluorene groups, i.e., H– (148), H–CF3 (149) and H–Ph (150), exhibited very low ASE thresholds (Table 4) and excellent thermal stabilities. The ASE thresholds of samples increased by less than 3-fold annealing up to 200 °C under ambient conditions. An only 5.6-fold threshold increase with annealing temperature up to 250 °C was recorded for H–F (148). The key data of DFB lasers are listed in Table 4.

284





Traditional starburst oligofluorenes usually contain a central core and multiple functionalized arms of oligofluorenes. Various cores, such as benzene,280 truxene,12,281,282 pyrene,47 and 1,4-diketo-2,3,5,6-tetraphenyl-pyrrolo[3,4-c]pyrrole (DPP),283 have been used to construct a variety of starburst oligofluorenes (151–166) as organic gain media. The selection of central cores is important to construct dendritic architectures. For example, as three overlapped fluorene fragments, truxene provide an alternative core structure to construct 2D starburst oligofluorenes (154–156). The oligomers consisting of the central truxene core and oligofluorene arms could be virtually considered as uniform starburst oligofluorenes with homogeneous fluorene units.240,284,285 Some other cores, such as triazatruxene,286 triphenylamine,287 pyrene,47 and DPP,283 which act as electron donors or acceptors, could strongly modulate the spectral and electrical properties of oligofluorenes arms. It should be noted that pyrene-cored starbursts or pyrene-capped starbursts were reported as attractive gain media because of the high PLQY, excellent thermal stability, and high charge carrier mobility of pyrene unit. The details will be discussed separately in the next section. In this section, we discuss the construction of a series of pyrene-cored gain media system by ICT through the oligofluorenes arms to pyrene core.
Three-arms oligofluorenes based on benzene and truxene were explored as gain media by Skabara and coworkers.280–282 The three-arms oligofluorenes materials based on benzene (B2, B3 and B4, 151–153) and truxene (T3 and T4, 154 and 155) are shown in Scheme 13. The truxene dendrimers exhibited lower ASE thresholds (T3 = 8.4 μJ cm−2, T4 = 16 μJ cm−2) compared with the benzene-cored dendritic oligofluorenes (B3 and B4 = 18 μJ cm−2). The truxene dendrimers exhibited high PLQYs in solid state (78–87%); while the benzene-cored counterparts showed the values ranging from 36% to 86%. Among them, T3 (154) exhibited the highest optical gain of 38 cm−1 (Table 4), which was only half of PFO gain value (74 cm−1).49 The combination of these two family materials allowed for broad wavelength tuning of 70 nm (402–473 nm) in the deep-blue spectral part for the DFB lasers. Interestingly, pure T3 (154) material achieved very broad lasing spectra (51 nm) from the 1D and 2D DFB lasers with a range of film thicknesses. The lowest lasing threshold was 2.06 μJ cm−2 for DFB T3 (154) lasers.281 Six-substituted truxene-based oligomers (Tr6-n, n = 1–4) demonstrated highly efficient blue emission (0.50–0.90) in the solid state. In these six-substituted oligomers, the enhanced steric hindrance between the arms and core and the consequently low π-conjugated degree led to blue-shifted absorption and emission, as compared with the three-armed analogues.12,240,288 Low-threshold 1D DFB lasers (1.3 μJ cm−2) with tunable wavelengths from 421 nm to 443 nm were demonstrated for the resulting starburst oligomers Tr6-3 (156).12
 Scheme 13  Dendritic starburst molecular structures based on oligofluorenes. 
In the electron donor/acceptor cored dendritic starburst systems, the absorbed energy can be transferred from the arms, which act as absorbing antennas, to the core through ICT. The typical examples were sky-blue multi-armed molecules based on pyrene as core and oligofluorenes as arms (Py-2 and Py-3, 157 and 158, Scheme 13).47,289 The sufficient spectral overlap between the absorption of pyrene core and the emission of oligofluorene arms facilitates energy transfer to the central pyrene core efficiently. Both Py-2 (157) and Py-3 (158) showed very high solid PLQYs (>90%), low ASE thresholds (Py-2: 1.75 μJ cm−2; Py-3: 1.78 μJ cm−2), relatively high gain values (75–78 cm−2), and excellent lasing performances. ASE thermal stabilities were measured to evaluate the gain properties of Py-2 (157) and Py-3 (158) at high working temperature. For Py-3 (158), the ASE threshold still kept at a low level even heated up to 200 °C. The ASE thermal stabilities of Py-2 (157) and Py-3 (158) were much better than that of their polymer counterpart, PFO, mainly due to the stable glassy structures and relatively stable amorphous rubber-like structures formed upon increasing the heating temperature. 1D DFB grating lasers were achieved with lasing thresholds down to 0.16 μJ cm−2, lasing slope efficiencies up to 3.9%, and ca. 40 nm wavelength tuning in the blue- and sky-blue-spectral region. Impressively, the non-encapsulated devices survived even operating at 10-times threshold energy with more than 1.1 × 105 pulses operation lifetime. Another reported type of dendritic starburst gain media through ICT was the functionalized starburst molecules based on DPP core with four oligofluorene arms (DPP-star, 159, as shown in Scheme 13).283 Different from the fully π-conjugated system in pyrene-cored starbursts (Py-2 and Py-3, 157 and 158), the DPP-star (159) had a π-conjugated axis and a non-conjugated axis. To understand the effects of conjugated pathway, linear molecules with DPP unit as the central core in the conjugated orientation (linear-DPP-c, 160, as shown in Scheme 13) and the non-conjugated orientation (linear-DPP-nc) were synthesized and investigated as well. DPP-star (159) and linear molecules exhibited PLQYs lower than 6% when excited at the oligofluorene absorption bands; while a PLQY up to 20% was achieved when excited in DPP-core absorbed spectral region. The insufficient spectral (and energetic) overlap between the absorption of DPP core and the emission of oligofluorenes arms led to a rather unsuccessful energy transfer from the oligofluorenes arms to the DPP core. DPP-star (159) molecule didn’t exhibited the presence of ASE; while spectral narrowing was observed for linear-DPP-c (160), in particular pumping DPP core directly. The ASE thresholds are 106 μJ cm−2 excited at 355 nm (pumping the oligofluorenes arms) and 42 μJ cm−2 excited at 532 nm (pumping the DPP core). Triazatruxene, an electron-rich unit, was also used as the core skeleton to construct three or six armed oligofluorenes (TAT-2, TAT-3, TAT6-2, TAT6-3, 161–164).286,290 Compared with the corresponding truxene-cored counterparts (T-n and Tr6-n), TAT-n (n = 2 and 3, 161 and 162) and TAT6-n (n = 2 and 3, 163 and 164) exhibited nearly 30 nm bathochromic shifts of the fluorescence spectra, suggesting ICT occurred between the arms and the core. Levermore et al. reported the ASE performance of triazatruxene-cored oligofluorenes.291 The six-armed oligofluorenes (TAT6-2 and TAT6-3, 163 and 164) exhibited low ASE thresholds (≤5 μJ cm−2), and ordinary gain values (around 25 cm−1).
Recently, Lai et al. reported a novel donor–acceptor core for dendritic starburst design, in which triazine with electron-withdrawing nature and favorable electron-transporting ability was selected as the center, and carbazole and diphenylamine units with electron-donating nature were attached onto the triazine center to modulate the electrical properties by the construction of heterogeneous donor–acceptor core structure.292–294 Different π-conjugated oligofluorenes were again selected as the arms to construct dendritic starbursts, named as TF-1 (165) and TF-2 (166). The chemical structures of TF-1 (165) and TF-2 (166) are shown in Scheme 13. The six-branched TF-1 and TF-2 exhibited fluorescence emission in deep-blue region with PLQYs over 40%. The ASE thresholds of TF-1 (165) and TF-2 (166) samples were recorded as 4.3 μJ cm−2 and 10.3 μJ cm−2, respectively. Remarkably, TF-1 (165) and TF-2 (166) manifested enhanced ASE stabilities without significant spectral shifts (within 2 nm for TF-1 (165) and 1 nm for TF-2 (166)) and obvious changed ASE thresholds annealing up to 200 °C in air.
3.2.3.2 Starbursts with pyrene as the central core and/or the end-cappers. 
Owing to high PLQY, excellent thermal stability, and high charge carrier mobility of pyrene, pyrene derivatives have attracted much interests.295 Pyrene derivatives with large steric hindrance have been demonstrated as optional candidates for lasing applications. Generally, planar pyrene molecules in solid state tend to form π–π stacking and excimers, accompanying with red-shifted emission and reduced fluorescent efficiencies. The likelihood of pyrene aggregation can be reduced by the introduction of bulky groups and/or constructing dendritic starburst structures. In this section, we introduce the recently reported pyrene-cored or pyrene-capped dendritic conjugated starbursts as efficient emitters for OSSLs.
In 2010, Uchimura et al. developed a series of star-shaped molecules using pyrene as the central core and benzene, diphenyl, terphenyl or anthracene units as the substituted arms, named as PyP-n (n = 1–4) (167–170) and PyA-m (m = 1, 2) (171 and 172).296 By doping these pyrene derivatives into cholesteric liquid crystals (CLCs), DFB lasers were constructed. In this study, the chain lengths [–(CH2)n–] of the alkyl groups were fixed at n = 6 and 7, which ensured good solubilities and ordered films in CLCs to achieve CLC DFB lasers. Interestingly, the resulting lasing thresholds were very different. Taken PyP-3 (169) and PyP-4 (170) as examples, a lasing threshold of PyP-4 (170) was recorded of 245 μJ cm−2, but the lasing signal of PyP-3 (169) could not be detected under the same optical density (OD, OD = 0.16). Due to its better solubility, PyP-4 (170) could achieve lower threshold for CLC lasers (Eth = 43.3 μJ cm−2) with much higher OD (OD = 1). The corresponding data are summarized in Table 4. This work indicated that the flexible chains could play a crucial role in the design of organic gain media, coinciding with our previous work.228 Triazatruxene was also introduced to link the planar pyrene core at 1,6- and 1,3,6,8-positions (Py-2TAT, and Py-4TAT, 173 and 174) to construct monodisperse starburst architectures.297 Compared with the reference linear counterpart (Py-2TAT, 173), Py-4TAT (174) showed a much higher PLQY (66% to 16%) resulted from the steric starburst molecular structure. But Py-4TAT (174) exhibited inferior ASE/lasing properties, mainly due to the detrimental ICT between pyrene core and four triazatruxene substituents.
In Section 3.2.3.1, pyrene-cored starburst oligofluorenes (Py-n, 157 and 158) were introduced as gain media, which had relatively excellent hole-injection abilities (the highest occupied molecular orbital, HOMO levels), high PLQYs, low lasing thresholds, net gain values of ca. 70 cm−1, and superior thermal and environmental stabilities.47 But the low electron-affinity of pyrene would reduce the current density and hinder the potential application in electrically pumped OSLs. Electron-donating (or electron-accepting) groups, e.g., cyanophenyl298 and diphenylamine units,299 were incorporated onto the pyrene-based starburst oligofluorenes (PyFnCN, n = 1–3, 175–177; PyFnNPh, n = 1–3, 178–180) to modulate the electrical properties. For OLED devices, PyFnCN (n = 1–3, 175–177) and PyFnNPh (n = 1–3, 178–180) manifested enhanced electrical properties with lower turn-on voltages, superior current efficiencies and brightness in comparison with the electroluminescent properties of their starburst counterparts Py-n (157 and 158) without electro-active end-cappers. Without sacrificing the optical properties, they showed comparable ASE/lasing thresholds and optical gain properties as summarized in Table 4.
Pyrenyl-capped dendritic starbursts were also synthesized and designed for lasing applications. For example, Lai et al. reported a set of novel trigonal starbursts with a truxene core and three end-capped pyrene units (TrF1Py and TrF2Py, 181 and 182).300 Due to the well-defined and restricted π-delocalization of truxene core, the fluorescence emission of TrF1Py (181) and TrF2Py (182) were blue-shifted compared with the pyrene-cored starbursts. The ASE thresholds for TrF1Py (181) and TrF2Py (182) films were recorded as 11.2 μJ cm−2 and 6.3 μJ cm−2, with the corresponding stimulated peak at 449 nm and 445 nm. TrF1Py (181) exhibited relatively stable ASE performance with only 4.5-fold Eth increment during the subsequent thermal annealing from room temperature to 250 °C. In comparison, Eth of TrF2Py (182) film increased seriously with 17.6-fold annealing up to 200 °C. For TrF2Py (182) film, green fluorescent emission band ranging from 500 nm to 600 nm appeared after annealing at 250 °C; while the PL spectrum remained almost unchanged without green emission band for TrF1Py (181) after annealing. The existence of green emission band, originated from the excimer formation (from the π–π stacking fluorene units), explains the different ASE behaviors of TrF1Py (181) and TrF2Py (182) after annealing. The key data obtained from DFB lasers with TrF1Py (181) and TrF2Py (182) as the emitters are summarized in Table 4. This work indicated that the long oligofluorene arms may be not the optimized choice for contributing dendritic starbursts. Due to superior ASE performance of TrF1Py (181), the pyrene-capped fluorene arms were further selected to construct stable organic gain media. Lai et al. constructed four-armed starburst macromolecules as gain media, with spirofluorene and pyrene as the central core.301 The chemical structures of the four-armed dendritic starbursts (PyFPy and SpFPy, 183 and 184) are depicted in Scheme 14. PL spectrum of PyFPy (183) showed bathochromic shift (>40 nm) compared with that of SpFPy (184) in film, due to the efficient energy transfer between pyrene-capped arms and pyrene core. The two samples manifested high PLQYs (74% for PyFPy, 183 and 62% for SpFPy, 184), low ASE thresholds (5.4 μJ cm−2 for PyFPy, 183 and 6.6 μJ cm−2 for SpFPy, 184) and stable ASE behaviors. ASE could still be detectable for the two samples even upon annealing up to 300 °C. To further estimate the optical stability, standard ASE measurements were conducted after soaking the samples in water (80 °C) for 10 min and then drying by blowing and annealing in nitrogen environment. The ASE thresholds of PyFPy (183) and SpFPy (184) showed slight increase with 2.6-fold and 4-fold after 7 test cycles, respectively. As a reference, even after the first cycle, the ASE threshold of PFO substantially increased with nearly 140-fold from 7.7 μJ cm−2 to 1.06 mJ cm−2. Therefore, PyFPy (183) and SpFPy (184) exhibited outstanding optical stabilities under both air and water conditions. The tunable lasing region of 30 nm (PyFPy (183): 476–505 nm; SpFPy (184): 442–472 nm) were achieved in DFB resonates by varying grating periods and emission layer thicknesses. The minimum lasing threshold values of 3.85 μJ cm−2 (24 nJ per pulse) and 6.0 μJ cm−2 (16 nJ per pulse) for PyFPy (183) and SpFPy (184) were obtained, respectively. Correspondingly, the DFB lasers based on PyFPy (183) and SpFPy (184) showed good ambient stabilities even under water conditions.
 Scheme 14  Conjugated starbursts with pyrene as the central core and/or the end-capper. 
3.2.3.3 Other dendritic starbursts. 
Generally, solid-state molecules suffer from concentration quenching, induced by intermolecular interactions. To address this challenge, multi-dimensional steric conformation based on spirobifluorene core was constructed to effectively prevent the molecular aggregation and improve the processability as well as the morphological stability. In 1998, Johansson et al. presented the spectral narrowing of blue-emitting spiro-linked oligophenylenes, named as Sp-P1 (185) and Sp-P2 (186),302 in amorphous films. The spectral narrowing with dramatic collapse (linewidths to 2.2–2.9 nm) were observed in Sp-P1 (185) and Sp-P2 (186) with an increase of pump fluence. The narrowing emission were associated with the 0–0 vibronic transition for Sp-P1 (185) and the 0–1 vibronic transition for Sp-P2 (186) in their emission spectra. Spehr et al. selected additional substitutions with four methoxy groups or two biphenyl groups attached onto spiro-sexiphenyl, named as Sp-P1 (185), Sp-P3 (187) and Sp-P4 (188).232 Four-carbazole-armed spiro-compound (Sp-Cz, 190) was also reported with ASE threshold down to 3 μJ cm−2 for the thin film prepared by vacuum vapor deposition. In 2005, Schneider et al. reported a spiro-material, 2,2′,7,7′-tetrakis(p-fluoro-phenyl)-9,9′-spirobifluorene (Sp-F, 189), as the emitter for thin-film UV OSSLs with a spectral range from 378 nm to 395 nm.303 DFB lasers based on this sample exhibited a minimum threshold of 62 μJ cm−2 at 382 nm. Output energy up to 6.8 W from the fabricated UV DFB laser was observed, rendering the resulting OSSL an efficient light source for biomarker spectroscopy. Minimum DFB lasing threshold was recorded as 0.32 J cm−2 at 478 nm from a guest–host optical gain system using 2,2′,7,7′-tetrakis(2,2-diphenylvinyl)spiro-9,9′-bi-fluorene (Sp-DPVBi, 191) as an emitter doping in another spiro molecule, 2,7-bis(biphenyl-4-yl)-2′w,7′-di-tert-butyl-9,9′-spirobi-fluorene, at a concentration of 1.1%.304
In 2015, Ma et al. reported a set of truxene-cored starburst molecules linked with different π-conjugated arms (TrSp, TrTSp, and TrCSp, 192–194).305Scheme 15 shows the molecular structures of these starburst compounds. The truxene core and spirobifluorene end-cappers were linked through single bonds (TrSp, 192), or thiophene units (TrTSp, 193), or N-ethylcarbazole units (TrCSp, 194). Truxene-cored starburst with spirobifluorene substituents (TrSp, 192) showed an optical gain with lasing fluence above 156 μJ cm−2. The ASE threshold came down to 83 μJ cm−2 for TrTSp (193) with thiophene linking truxene core and spirobifluorene substituents. But ASE disappeared in TrCSp (194) with thiophene replaced by N-ethylcarbazole. The improved intermolecular vibronic coupling and suppressed low-frequency vibrational modes via incorporating the thiophene units were presented with density functional theory (DFT) calculation. The enhanced high-frequency modes and decreased low-frequency modes are favorable for the formation of effective quasi four-level energy system for stimulated emission. On the contrary, the embedded N-ethylcarbazole moieties induce abundant strongly elongated low-frequency modes, which ruin the quasi four-level energy system.
 Scheme 15  Other dendritic starbursts as organic gain media. 
A series of structural isomers of trigonal starburst molecules, containing a truxene core and quarter-dialkylfluorene arms with different locations of benzothiadiazole (BT) moiety (Tr-nBT, n = 1–5, 195–199), were presented by Skabara and Bradley.306 The optical properties from absorption and PL spectra could be adequately described in accordance with BT unit at the three different possible positions, corresponding to a Tr-1/4BT pair (195 and 198), a Tr-2/3BT pair (196 and 197) and Tr-5BT (199), rationalized on the basis of molecular symmetry. Dramatically, Tr-5BT (199) exhibited a very weak BT unit absorption, weak BT Raman scattering intensities centered at 1360 and 1547 cm−1, and a strong blue-shifted PL, as a direct consequence of the relatively weak amplitude of HOMO with occupied natural transition orbital (NTO) when BT unit is located at the arm end. It led to the formation of a fast relaxed excited state from the Franck–Condon (vertical transition) state created by photon absorption, which resulted in the absence of ASE even having a pumping energy of up to 500 μJ cm−2, and the accompanying low PLQY (ca. 27%). For Tr-nBT (n = 1–4, 195–198), the ASE thresholds were around 60 μJ cm−2, and the ASE saturation were observed above 200 μJ cm−2. The results indicated that any effect of changing the ground state polarization gradient may affect the performance of gain media in ASE and lasing. Recently, Lai et al. reported a novel set of monodisperse ladder-type oligo(p-phenylene)s starbursts, named as TrL-n (n = 1–3, 200–202).284 Compared with their linear counterparts, TrL-n (200–202) exhibited suppressed low-energy emission and enhanced optical stabilities. The rather low ASE thresholds, i.e., threshold of 1.60 μJ cm−2 for TrL-3 (202), were recorded for TrL-n, suggesting their promising prospects for use as organic gain media.


3.2.4 Conjugated polymers. 
Fluorescent polymers with π-conjugation are yielded from various monomers, ranging from randomly composed chains to highly oriented and defined chains. Polymers can possess various morphologies varying from disorderly amorphous to highly oriented crystalline. As far as we are concerned, the amorphous polymers usually exhibit good optical properties, while the crystalline polymers often manifest superior charge transport properties.
Particularly, polymers exhibit excellent film-forming abilities and attractive mechanical properties, thus rendering them highly attractive for flexible optoelectronic devices via solution processing.
3.2.4.1 Polyfluorenes. 
Polyfluorenes and their derivatives have been served as a significant kind of polymers for application in OSSLs. Various metastable morphologies are observed in polyfluorenes, and the specific morphology greatly relies on the fabrication process and/or the thermal history of the molecular film layer. There are a number of studies on the phases and photophysical properties of polyfluorenes with different side-substitutions. Among them, PFO (203) is one of the most widely known conjugated polymers, demonstrating particularly rich-phase behaviors. Among the fluorene-based polymers, the lowest lasing thresholds by far were recorded for those with β-phase morphology, in which individual oriented molecular chain had an extended π-conjugation without π–π stacking.307 Recent studies on the refractive index deviation of β-phase and amorphous phase polyfluorenes showed that attractive device geometries featuring with mixed morphologies can be constructed.308 In comparison to the amorphous morphology, the β-phase PFO (203) demonstrated an additional lower energy absorption shoulder (∼430 nm), resulted in a 1–2% refractive index increase in this spectral region. DFB lasers based on PFO (203) in β-phase via spin-coating manifested bathochromic shifts by a few nanometers compared with the output lasing from the amorphous polymer counterpart integrating with the same period DFB gratings.309
PFO (203) with various morphologies could be induced via spin-coating from various solvents, thermal cycling, exposure to solvent vapors, etc. Liquid crystalline phases could be processed by depositing PFO (203) onto the rubbed polyimide substrates.310 By nanoimprinting, DFB grating was imposed onto the PFO (203) layer. The wavelengths of lasing emission and the lasing thresholds could be tuned by controlling the PFO (203) chains orientation with respect to the nanoimprinted 1D DFB grating direction. In this way, the effective refractive indexes of PFO (203) could be adjusted to the oscillation geometry and lasing thresholds as low as 20 μJ cm−2 could be achieved. On the other hand, the polymer alignment could improve the charge carrier mobility. The combination of β-phase and amorphous-phase morphology of PFO (203) layer were achieved by spin-coating hot solution and then exposing in toluene vapor. Side-by-side PFO (203) layer with various morphologies were induced onto the grating.311 The metastable morphology could be eliminated by heating up to the transition temperature under the subsequent solvent exposure. Alexander et al. reported a novel polyfluorene with vinyl-ether-functionalized aliphatic side-chains (VE-PFO, 204).307,312 An extraordinary advantage of this polyfluorene was that the induced β-phase morphology could be fixed by cross-linking. The crosslinked VE-PFO (204) film don’t dissolve in the subsequent solvent processing without affecting the morphology patterns. With this strategy, a β-phase cross-linkable VE-PFO (204) layer was imposed with a DFB grating by using electron beam lithography. As followed, an amorphous layer of VE-PFO (204) on top was formed by spin-coating, resulting in a double-layered device with a stack of β-phase and amorphous morphologies. Both layers emitted laser radiation at the same time in such a layered device with mixed morphologies when the excitation energy densities exceeded these two thresholds. A lasing threshold as low as 0.4 μJ cm−2 was achieved from the β-phase cross-linkable VE-PFO (204) film. Recently, Lin et al. synthesized a model polyfluorene, namely, poly[4-(octyloxy)-9,9-diphenylfluorene-2,7-diyl]-co-[5-(octyloxy)-9,9-diphenylfluoren-2,7-diyl] (PODPF, 205), in which the octyloxy unit lied at 4-position of fluorene and diphenyl moieties substituted at the 9-position of fluorene units.313 The β-phase morphology of PODPF (205) film was achieved by spin-coating from toluene solution, and erased by the annealing process. The thin film of PODPF (205) exhibited outstanding spectral stability without obviously green fluorescent emission band even annealing at 200 °C for 12 h under ambient conditions. The ASE threshold of β-phase PODPF (205) for 461 nm was 15.7 μJ cm−2, and the lasing peak at 462 nm with a threshold of 42 nJ per pulse was achieved.
Modulating the lengths of side flexible chain has been proved to be an effective strategy to finely modulate the charge carrier mobilities of polyfluorenes. For example, dioctylfluorene was copolymerized with diethylhexylfluorene to improve the hole mobility of polyfluorene, and thus a homopolymer involving only dioctylfluorene (PFO, 203), and copolymers consisting of dioctylfluorene and diethylhexyl-fluorene with ratios of 4:1 (PFO-EH-1, 206) and 1:1 (PFO-EH-2, 207) were synthesized.11 The molecular structures of these polymers are depicted in Fig. 12a and Scheme 16. Decreasing the length of substituted flexible chain from octyl to butyl, the interchain distance was reduced with increased mobility and favorable light emission characteristic. The ASE wavelengths were around 450 nm for all polymers (Fig. 12b). As shown in Fig. 12c, the resulting copolymer materials exhibited enhanced hole mobilities (determined by time-of-flight (TOF)) (μhole ≈ 3–6 × 10−4 cm2 V−1 s−1), which were more than 100-fold higher than that of PFO (203). The increased mobilities were resulted from improved interchain charge hopping at the sites of diethylhexylfluorene monomer, where the distance between intermolecular polyfluorene skeletons was improved, compared with those with octyl-substituents that might isolate the polyfluorene chains. With 1D DFB grating, a lasing threshold (0.3 μJ cm−2) with a slope efficiency of 11% (Fig. 12d) was obtained for PFO-ET-1 (206) as gain media. Organic lasers with a 45 nm spectral window from 434 nm to 479 nm were achieved by varying the film thicknesses (45–160 nm) (Fig. 12e).
 Fig. 12  (a) Polymer chemical structures of PFO-EH-1, PFO-EH-2 and PFO. (b) Absorbance (solid line, left), PL (dash line, right) and ASE (filled area, right) spectra for PFO-EH-1 (red), PFO-EH-2 (green) and PFO (black). (c) Field dependence of the mobility for PFO-EH-1 (red), PFO-EH-2 (green) and PFO (black). (d) Input–output characteristics for a 1D DFB laser with covered PFO-EH-1 film. The right inset shows output emission spectra with the increase of pump energy. (e) Top panel: The spectral window of organic lasers. Bottom panel: Corresponding threshold variation of the output lasing wavelength for the PFO-EH-1 DFB lasers. Reproduced with permission from ref. 11. Copyright 2008 Nature Publishing Group. 
 Scheme 16  Polyfluorene derivatives as gain media. 
For blue-color-emitting polyfluorene polymers, one serious issue is their poor spectral stabilities caused by interchain interactions or the oxidative instabilities with fluorenone defects. Great efforts, such as constructing copolymer configuration, the introduction of other functional groups, spiro linkages, etc., have been made to overcome this challenge. Xia et al. reported a copolymer, poly(9,9-dioctyl-fluorene-co-9,9-di(4-methoxy)phenylfluorene) (PF8DP, 208) as gain medium,314 which exhibited similar PL spectrum, lower ASE threshold (6.25 μJ cm−2), and similar gain value (66 cm−1) compared with PFO (203). A novel polymer with spiroanthracenefluorene monomer, namely poly(9-spiro(10,10-bis(2-ethylhexyl)-10H-anthracene)-fluorene) (PEHSAF, 209), was reported as gain medium, which exhibited thermally stable stimulated emission in vacuum upon annealing up to 250 °C with moderate gain coefficient (38 cm−1) and ASE threshold (131 μJ cm−2).315 Functional groups, such as carbazole,316 bi-naphthalene,317 solfonyl,50 and dibenzothiophene-S,S-dioxide spacer units,318 were selected to construct statistical copolymers of polyfluorenes. The polymer structures of PFC (210), PFBN (211), PFSO (212) and PPFSO-n (n = 1–3) (213–215) are shown in Scheme 16. The corresponding data of ASE/lasing performance are shown in Table 5. Polyspirobifluorenes cross-linked with the use of oxetane functional groups were also explored and investigated, which exhibited mediocre ASE performance.319–321

3.2.4.2 LPPPs. 
The low carrier mobilities and poor oxidative stabilities in polyfluorene derivatives, which is generally caused by interchain-aggregation-induced energy transfer to fluorenone defects, have inevitably impeded their potential applications in electrically driven OSSLs. To overcome this problem, LPPPs with the design concept to fuse the conjugated backbone architectures have been proposed to explore organic emitters with great thermal and electrochemical stability, and prominent charge-carrier mobility. The investigation of synthesis and photophysical property of LPPP was firstly reported by Scherf and Müllen in 1991.322 Since then, a large number of ladder-type polymers have been designed and synthesized for organic electronics.323,324 Among them, the most well-known ladder-type polymer is MeLPPP (217), which has aroused intense photophysical investigations.46,60,322,325–327 It showed an ASE threshold of 20 μJ cm−2 at the peak of 489 nm. The bathochromic-shifted ASE peak, compared with PFO (203), was mainly due to the large planar rigid ladder-type conjugated backbone structure. A moderate gain coefficient of 16 cm−1 was detected by using VSL method in MeLPPP (217). Kallinger et al. reported the lasing performance based on MeLPPP (217) as the emitter. A low lasing threshold (4.8 μJ cm−2) was demonstrated.325
In 2008, Laquai et al. demonstrated the ASE performance of a number of blue-emitting LPPPs, namely, LPPP-1/2/3 (218–220).46 Carbazole was introduced to construct ladder-type polymers (PLC-1/2, 221 and 222). A fully arylated polyfluorene (PFP8, 216, as shown in Scheme 16) was selected as a reference for the comparison with the ladder-type polymers. The chemical structures of LPPP-1/2/3 (218–220) and PLC-1/2 (221 and 222) are depicted in Scheme 17. The ASE thresholds of carbazole-containing LPPPs (PLC-1/2, 221 and 222) were much higher, and no meaningful gain coefficient was recorded for the resulting carbazole-containing polymers. LPPP-1 (218), whose monomer with 3 bridged phenyl rings, demonstrated a low ASE threshold of 3 μJ cm−2 and a relatively high optical gain of 21 cm−1. As to indenofluorene copolymers, ASE thresholds close to that of LPPP-1 (218) were observed for poly(indenofluorene-cophenanthrene)s (4.5 μJ cm−2 and 17.5 μJ cm−2 for PIF-1 and PIF-2, respectively; 223 and 224).48 However, PIF-1 (223) demonstrated a very high gain value of 154 cm−1. More impressively, a TOF hole mobility of 10−2 cm2 V−1 s−1 was obtained for PIF-1 (223). In an OFET architecture, hole mobility up to 0.5 cm2 V−1 s−1 was achieved. Simultaneous optimization of stimulated emission and charge carrier mobility is generally required towards electrically pumped organic lasers.
 Scheme 17  Ladder-type poly(p-phenylene)s as gain media. 
3.2.4.3 Polymers contain BT units. 
As for other polyfluorene copolymers, poly(dioctylfluorene-co-benzothiadiazole) (F8BT, 225) is a popular conjugated polymer with yellow-green stimulated emission and moderate ASE threshold (28 μJ cm−2).314 By modulating the ratio of fluorene (F8) to BT units from 1:1 to 9:1, the ASE threshold of this novel material, named as 9F8–1BT (226), was significantly decreased with a value of 2 μJ cm−2.328 This improvement might originate from the isolation of polymer chains by the octyl side chains, leading to the efficiently suppressed exciton annihilation. With using 9F8–1BT (226) as the emitter, the first LED pumped OSSL was demonstrated by Samuel's group.19 The schematic diagram of the hybrid InGaN-semiconducting polymer laser is depicted in Fig. 1. The onset of DFB laser at 568 nm was achieved when the InGaN LED driving current above 144 A, corresponding to a lasing threshold of 6.5 μJ cm−2 (180 W cm−2). This work opened the gate of indirect electrically pumped organic lasers.18,26,27,125–127 With using F8BT (225) and 9F8–1BT (226) as gain media, Karl et al. recently reported the membrane-based, substrate-less and extremely thin DFB lasers for portable security tags application.329
Recently, Yu et al. developed a series of fluorene-based copolymers with yellow-green emission composed of the branched 2-methyl-butyl substituted fluorene-co-benzothiadiazole and F8BT monomer in different ratios (namely, nF1/4F8BT, 227–230, with n representing the ratio of 9,9-di(2-methyl)butyl (F1/4) in the copolymer).330 The chemical structures of nF1/4F8BT are depicted in Scheme 18. Doping nF1/4F8BT (227–230) copolymers into PFO (203), a systematic increase in PLQY was observed by increasing the ratio of the F1/4 monomers. Manifested as reduced ASE thresholds (2.8 μJ cm−2vs. 4.3 μJ cm−2) and increased gain values (32 cm−1vs. 22 cm−1) with respect to F8BT blends, nF1/4F8BT (227–230) blends exhibited superior optical gain properties. The branched F1/4 substitution in the copolymers hindered exciton annihilation. Ladder-type oligophenylenes were introduced to construct ladder-type poly(indenofluorene-co-benzothiadiazole)s to explore light amplified application.331,332 Ladder-type oligo(indenofluorene)s were incorporated with BT units and various indenofluorene chains with different lengths, denoted as LPBT-n (n = 1–3) (231–233). Compared with 9,9-dioctylfluorene units, the indenofluorene chains exhibited much more efficient energy transfer to BT units. Without blending, a low ASE threshold of 6.0 μJ cm−2 was achieved for LPBT-3 (233), with a high net gain value (84 cm−1).
 Scheme 18  Polymeric gain media with BT units. 
Lasing can also be observed in ternary copolymers. Ternary copolymer poly(fluorene-co-benzothiadiazole-co-thiophene-co-tri-phenylamine), known as Red F (234), was reported as a gain medium.314,333,334 A very broad tunable wavelength range (627–702 nm) was demonstrated in the resulting optically pumped 2D DFB lasers.333 The lowest lasing threshold was recorded of 1.1 μJ cm−2 for the operation at 698 nm. Optical switching in DFB lasers based on Red F (234) with red lasing emission was demonstrated by Xia et al., using optical control pulses (1.28 μm, 4 ps, 5 kHz).334 As a response to the optical control pulse, the reduction of the lasing output by more than 30 times can be used a new switch to provide effective wavelength conversion from silica to polymer optical fiber to regulate the communication wavelengths.
3.2.4.4 PPV derivatives. 
Besides the fluorene-based polymers, another important class of linear polymers are PPVs. The first polymer light-emitting diode (PLED) was reported based on PPV (235) by Friend and co-authors in 1990.5 Lasing emission from the polymer film was reported in 1996 by optically pumping PPV (235).17 Due to the insoluble nature of PPV (235), PPV (235) films were usually fabricated from the solution-processable precursor polymer. The precursor polymer converted into PPV (235) in films after thermal conversion.5,335 Various flexible chains were then added onto the phenylene units to enhance the polymeric solubility. Various PPV derivatives were reported with promising ASE/lasing performance, including poly(2-butyl-5-(2′-ethylhexyl)-1,4-phenylenevinylene) (BuEH-PPV, 236),17 poly(2,5-bis(2′,5′-bis(2′′-ethylhexyloxy)phenyl)-1,4-phenylenevinylene) (BBEHP-PPV, 237),336,337 poly(2-methoxy-5-(2′-ethylhexyloxy)-1,4-phenylenevinylene) (MEH-PPV, 238),338 poly((2-(2′,5′-bis(2′′-ethylhexyloxy)phenyl)-1,4-phenylene-vinylene)-co-(2-methoxy-5-(2′-ethyl-hexyloxy)-1,4-phenylenevinylene)) (BEHP-co-MEH-PPV, 239),339,340etc. The polymer structures are depicted in Scheme 19.
 Scheme 19  PPV derivatives for polymer lasers. 
“Super Yellow” (240) is one of the most popular PPV derivatives as yellow emitters in PLEDs.341,342 PPV derivatives BBEHP-PPV (237) and MEH-PPV (238) have been extensively studied for green and red polymer lasers, because of their very low ASE/lasing thresholds and high optical gain values. For example, Rose et al. recorded the low lasing threshold of BBEHP-PPV (237) down to 0.04 μJ cm−2 (Fig. 13a–c), and demonstrated the sensing application to detect explosives based on the resulting organic laser device.336 The results showed that the resulting optically pumped attenuated laser device based on BBEHP-PPV (237) film displayed excellent sensitivity to the explosive vapors over 30 times higher, compared with the observation from PL emission (Fig. 13d and f). As sketched in Fig. 13e, in the existence of explosive vapors, the DFB lasing threshold increased and the chemical sensitivity was determined. Non-radiative deactivation pathways were induced by trace amount of explosive vapors of 2,4,6-trinitrotoluene (TNT) and 2,4-dinitrotoluene (DNT), leading to the competition with stimulated emission. In other work, by mixing BBEHP-PPV (237) with 1,4-cyclohexanedimethanol divinyl ether (CHDV) and spin-coating onto a DFB grating, the diluted BBEHP-PPV (237) film exhibited a high threshold of 1.1 mJ cm−2.337
 Fig. 13  (a) Chemical structures of BBEHP-PPV, TNT and DNT. (b) DFB grating structure fabricated from polydimethylsiloxane. (c) Lasing spectra of BBEHP-PPV atop a DFB structure. (d and f) Spectral sensitivity of optically pumping BBEHP-PPV thin film before and after exposure to DNT under different pulse energies (0.39 μJ cm−2, d; 0.96 μJ cm−2, f). (e) Schematic representation of ASE increase in gain medium after exposure to quencher. Reproduced with permission from ref. 336. Copyright 2005 Nature Publishing Group. 
Park et al. investigated the ASE performance of MEH-PPV (238) in 1999.343 Narrowed spectrum was recorded with an ASE threshold of 62.5 μJ cm−2 and emission center at 638 nm. The net gain of doped MEH-PPV film is ca. 70 cm−1.344 Since then, MEH-PPV (238) has been widely used as a standard red-color gain medium in organic laser physics. For example, a 2D DFB laser based on MEH-PPV (238) was demonstrated by Samuel and co-authors.345 A pumping energy threshold of 5.9 μJ cm−2 was recorded by the observation of the band-edge lasing at 636 nm. The laser exhibited an energy slope efficiency of 6.8%, with maximum output energy of 1.12 nJ per pulse at pumping energy of 20.4 nJ per pulse. A novel copolymer, named as BEHP-co-MEH-PPV (239), consisting of the monomers of BEHP-PPV and MEH-PPV, was found to be resistant to photodegradation with good solubility at room temperature. PL spectrum of BEHP-co-MEH-PPV (239) covered broad emission ranging from 550 nm to 625 nm, corresponding to the yellow-red emission characteristic of MEH-PPV, indicating efficient intramolecular energy transfer in the polymer skeletons. A low lasing threshold was achieved (9 μJ cm−2) in a single-defect microcavity structure, with emission peak at 607 nm.340
3.2.4.5 Other polymeric gain media. 
Hyperbranched polymers have been employed as efficient emitters in OLEDs,346,347 especially in white PLEDs.348,349 The molecular design provides a valuable platform to achieve high-quality white EL. As a part of the efforts, Lai et al. explored the fine modulation of the energy transfer process in different emission species of two novel star-shaped single-polymer systems.350,351 Two star-shaped models, tris(4-(3-hexyl-5-(7-(4-hexylthiophen-2-yl)-benzo[c][1,2,5]thiadiazol-4-yl)thiophen-2-yl)phenyl)amine (TN) and 7,7′-(5,5′-(9-(4-(3-hexyl-5-(7-(4-hexyl-thiophen-2-yl)benzo[c][1,2,5]thiadiazol-4-yl)thiophen-2-yl)phenyl)-H-carbazole-3,6-diyl)bis(4-hexylthiophene-5,2-diyl))bis(4-(4-hexylthiophen-2-yl)benzo[c][1,2,5]thiadiazole) (TM), were chosen as the central cores for saturated red emission. Star-shaped single polymers were framed by introducing BT units as green emissive dopants and polyfluorene as blue emissive moieties onto the red TN/TM cores. The chemical structures of star-shaped single-polymers (241–250) with different ratios of TN/TM and BT units are depicted in Scheme 20. Interestingly, ASE could be observed in these white emissive single-polymers. Due to the trace amounts of green and red emission units, the incomplete energy transfers could be achieved by ICT in these white emissive single-polymers. The ASE peaks were located at 448 nm, which were assigned to the stimulated emission of polyfluorenes. The large optical losses induced by abundant vibronic bands from the broad PL emission in these polymers led to much higher ASE thresholds (>10 fold) as compared with PFO (203).
 Scheme 20  Polyfluorene derivatives as gain media. 
Generally, physical blending strategy is an effective method to improve the optical gain properties. Mixing two different chromophores could effectively prevent concentration quenching. With this strategy, some less emissive materials could achieve relatively low ASE/lasing thresholds. For example, for thiophene-based polymers, lasing was recorded in microcavity resonator containing poly(3-(2,5-dioctylphenyl)thiophene) (PDOPT, 251) with relatively high lasing threshold of 2.4 μJ cm−2 (24 mW cm−2).352,353 But no lasing action was observed for poly(3-hexylthiophene) (P3HT, 252) even in high Q resonator configuration.354 By blending 10–20 wt% P3HT (252) with F8BT (225) films, efficient lasing from P3HT (252) with attractive optical gain properties were achieved.355 A threshold of ca. 60 μJ cm−2 with a corresponding high gain (50 cm−1) was recorded for the blended film with 15 wt% P3HT (252). The resulting 1D DFB lasers exhibited thresholds as low as 26 μJ cm−2, much lower than other thiophene-based polymers.
As discussed in Section 2.4.3, MOFs were selected as feedback structures for organic dye lasers because of their quasi-periodic configurations. However, the device stability is a key issue because of the short-term stability of the encapsulated dye. Chemically bridging the metal-nodes with chromophore-linkers would enhance the thermal stability compared to the physically doping strategy. Recently, Medishetty et al. reported two novel 3D MOFs, In-MOF (253) and Zn-MOF (254), which were derived from a well-established AIE dye, tetrakis[4-(4-carboxyphenyl)phenyl]ethane (H4TCPE), acting as a linker coordinating with In3+ and Zn2+ nodes (Fig. 14a and b).103 Pumped by femtosecond pulse laser, these MOF single crystals generated intrinsic blue stimulated emission with resulted ASE thresholds of 36 mJ cm−2 and 27 mJ cm−2 for In-MOF (253) and Zn-MOF (254), respectively (Fig. 14c, d and f). Besides, the emission lifetime of In-MOF (253) decreased from 900 ns to 500 ns for stimulated emission (Fig. 14e). Similarly, the decreased fluorescence lifetime from 1.2 ms to 380 ns was also shown in Zn-MOF (254) (Fig. 14g). It should be noted that there was no lasing occurring during the optical pumping, though in some cases, the MOF quasi-periodic structure could act as a Fabry–Perot cavity to realize lasing.100–102 The ASE thresholds were relatively higher compared with other organic materials. But still, this work proved that coordination network materials, such as MOFs, could act as novel optical gain media for lasing applications.
 Fig. 14  (a and b) Perspective views of In-MOF (a) and Zn-MOF (b) molecular structures. (c) PL and ASE optical images of Zn-MOF crystals with the scale bar of 100 μm. (d and f) Dependence of emission spectrum on pumping laser energy for In-MOF (d) and Zn-MOF (f). (e and g) Transient PL with pumping energy below and above ASE threshold in In-MOF (e) and Zn-MOF (g). Reproduced with permission from ref. 103. Copyright 2017 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 

3.3 Triplet gain media and triplet sensitizers
Generally, there are mainly three emission mechanisms for organic light sources: fluorescence, phosphorescence,356 and TADF,2 currently available for achieving efficient light output. The existing organic gain media are dominated by fluorescent materials.
According to spin statistics, a 1:3 ratio for singlet and triplet excitons is expected in carrier recombination,357,358 indicating that 75% formed excitons are prevented for the contribution of the singlet population in fluorescent emitters. As a result, a maximum of 25% for internal electroluminescence (EL) quantum efficiency (IQE) would be limited in organic light-emitting devices, such as OLEDs, under current injection. Phosphorescent and TADF materials are particularly interesting, in which theoretically all of the formed excitons, including singlet and triplet excitons, could be harvested as EL. As discussed in Section 4, extremely high current density (a few kA cm−2) is necessary for achieving electrically pumped OSSLs. Therefore, if the 75% triplet excitons could be effectively utilized, it would be much helpful to reduce the injected current density above lasing threshold for electrically pumped OSSLs. In most common cases, since the value of the excited-state absorption cross-section (σex, T1 → Tn) for phosphorescent (or TADF) emitter is much larger than the maximum stimulated emission cross-section (σem, T1 → S0), no ASE occurs in the pumping process.44
The only one example of phosphorescence laser was reported by Yu et al.40 Unlike most phosphorescent metal-complex molecules, the phosphorescence was achieved from a sulfide-substituted difluoroboron compound, (E)-3-(((4-nitrophenyl)imino)methyl)-2H-thiochroman-4-olate·BF2 (S-BF2, 255) (Scheme 21 and Fig. 15a). Compared with the non-substituted compound, (E)-2-(((4-nitrophenyl)vimino)methyl)-naphthalen-1-olate·BF2 (C-BF2) (Fig. 15a), S-BF2 (255) reversed the lowest singlet π–π* electronic transition by n–π* transition. In sharp contrast to the forbiddance of ISC from S1(π,π*) to T1(π,π*) in C-BF2, T1(π,π*) ← S1(n,π*) ISC was found with 100% efficiency in S-BF2 (255). ICT character was introduced in S-BF2 (255) with the assistance of the nitro moiety, leading to a moderate phosphorescence efficiency of 10%. S-BF2 (255) single-crystalline nanowires were fabricated via solution self-assembly method. As shown in Fig. 15b and c, the nanowires manifested strong yellow-red emission and acted as effective optical waveguide with bright PL from the end tips. A fast decay lifetime of 2.3 ns was monitored with PL peak at 510 nm (Fig. 15d), corresponding to fluorescence emission. Another emission peak of S-BF2 (255) nanowire was at 610 nm with a decayed lifetime of 9.3 μs (Fig. 15d), which was assigned to phosphorescence emission. Lasing output at 650 nm was recorded in the 30 μm length nanowire, mainly because of the construction of Fabry–Perot microcavity in the nanowire (Fig. 15e). The evolution from phosphorescence radiation to narrowed spontaneous emission started at 10.4 μJ cm−2, and further increasing the pumping energy above 12.8 μJ cm−2, lasing oscillation occurred (Fig. 15f and Table 6). FWHM of individual cavity mode was ca. 0.76 nm with lasing peak at 650 nm, obtaining a cavity quality factor Q ≈ 850. More modes in the nanowire lasing spectra were observed by increasing the nanowire lengths (Fig. 15g). A linear relationship was demonstrated in the value of mode spacing dependent on 1/L (L is the twice fold of nanowire length). This work made a big leap by exploring phosphorescence gain media for addressing the challenges towards OSL diodes (OSLDs).
 Scheme 21  The triplet gain media. 
 Fig. 15  (a) Chemical structures and energy level diagrams of S-BF2 and C-BF2. (b) PL microscopy image of S-BF2 nanowires. (c) Absorption and PL spectra of S-BF2 nanowire. (d) Transient PL of S-BF2 nanowire monitored at the fluorescence region (red, inset) and the phosphorescence region (black). (e) Emission spectra under different pump densities in a single 38 μm length S-BF2 nanowire. (f) Dependence of output intensity on pumping density in the fluorescence band and the phosphorescence band, monitored at 510 nm and 650 nm, respectively. (g) High-resolution lasing spectra for S-BF2 nanowires with lengths of 20, 30, and 50 μm. Reproduced with permission from ref. 40. Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society.
